<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_398477307">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/398477307" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10992379-aaron-dembski-bowden" class="left imgcol" title="Aaron Dembski-Bowden"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442458672p2/10992379.jpg" alt="Aaron Dembski-Bowden" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/398477307?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10992379-aaron-dembski-bowden" class="user" title="Aaron Dembski-Bowden">Aaron Dembski-Bowden</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer398477307">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6227048533354056854">This was one of those great moments where you come across a book that feels like it was specifically written for you. <br /><br />I finished The Winter King, turned the last page, and went to make some coffee. The first thing I did when I headed back upstairs was open it at page 1 and start it again. <br /><br />I'm not sure what I can say about this that'll actually do it justice. The characters' journeys through the story were variously gripping, heart-wrenching and tense, whether on the battlefield, travelling betwe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6227048533354056854">This was one of those great moments where you come across a book that feels like it was specifically written for you. <br /><br />I finished The Winter King, turned the last page, and went to make some coffee. The first thing I did when I headed back upstairs was open it at page 1 and start it again. <br /><br />I'm not sure what I can say about this that'll actually do it justice. The characters' journeys through the story were variously gripping, heart-wrenching and tense, whether on the battlefield, travelling between towns, or spending months inside cities suffering siege, or waiting for negotiations to end. I wasn't bored for a second. I didn't skip a single sentence to flip ahead to something more interesting, which I'm occasionally guilty of elsewhere. <br /><br />No character was uniformly detestable or utterly noble - it was much too real and well-written for that. Everyone had some realistic foible or flaw that affected them (and the wider narrative), and everyone had a journey of ups, downs and side-to-sides. Rather than build to a crescendo, it rose to several along the way and saved the best for last, when practically every character came together in a massive, massive nexus to decide the fate of the kingdom.<br /><br />The Dark Age magic (druid rituals, and the Isle of the Dead) was handled with a balance of gritty mystery and understandable displays of superstition from those in its presence. The battles were brutal - not quite as unforgivingly visceral as, say, Steven Pressfield - but no less emotional and evocative. They were just fantastic. Similarly, the romance, friendships and relationships were unfailingly portrayed as totally believable and real, each with moments of awkwardness, unreserved intimacy, warmth and betrayal. <br /><br />The entire book bled atmosphere and immersion, showing the world in all its filthy, earthy glory. A Britain fallen into disrepair and darkness, with the unwanted light of Roman civilisation finally gone. Echoes of their influence remain, but their craftsmanship, architecture and road-building can no longer be recreated by the warring tribes that call the land home. Instead of hearkening back to the Romans as bringers of culture and light, the superstitious Britons curse them as often as admire them (for obvious reasons...)<br /><br />I loved it, cover to cover.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6227048533354056854">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer443188401">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7407614684201404825">Four with a push.<br /><br />His post-Rome Britain is as much a battleground of religions as of peoples; Arthur seems to be the secular arm of Merlin’s fight. I felt a homage to T.H. White in the extended description of Merlin’s eccentric home, and in the comedy he can make of a scene – but that might just be me. The druidry is truly weird and very violent; Christianity has the book stacked against it, a bit too obvio</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7407614684201404825">Four with a push.<br /><br />His post-Rome Britain is as much a battleground of religions as of peoples; Arthur seems to be the secular arm of Merlin’s fight. I felt a homage to T.H. White in the extended description of Merlin’s eccentric home, and in the comedy he can make of a scene – but that might just be me. Nimue is Merlin’s worthy offsider, with a fierce, single-minded commitment to their fight. The druidry is truly weird and very violent; Christianity has the book stacked against it, a bit too obviously, I’m afraid.<br /><br />As others have said, he sets himself up a problem with Arthur, in that, for instance, Lancelot’s a turd but Arthur can’t be allowed to see he is, so he comes across as unperceptive. Also Arthur has to do things against his principles, or let things be done, quite regularly until you start to not want to hear about his principles, then. I liked his portrayal at first but was less and less convinced. <br /><br />I enjoyed most the little dramas set inside the book: the siege of King Ban’s ideal island Ynys Trebes; the Isle of the Dead – with description of these sites. He always has in mind where his scenes are set and makes them real.<br /><br />Was turned off at the end...<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[I felt I was invited to enter into and enjoy the bloody revenge.     <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_678045180">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/678045180" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7177008-jane" class="left imgcol" title="Jane"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1388366904p2/7177008.jpg" alt="Jane" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/678045180?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 29, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7177008-jane" class="user" title="Jane">Jane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=king-arthur" class="actionLinkLite">king-arthur</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer678045180">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12307575115120149741">Forget *any* preconceptions of Arthur that you may have gleaned from previous reading--from the Arthurian romances to straight historical fiction. Remember the names but no previous notions. This is Cornwell's unique concept of the Arthurian characters. It took me about a quarter of the novel to get used to his portrayals; but once I did, I was engrossed.<br /><br />Derfel, formerly one of Arthur's warriors, is an old man and has become a monk. He, with the help and questions of the present Queen of Powys,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12307575115120149741">Forget *any* preconceptions of Arthur that you may have gleaned from previous reading--from the Arthurian romances to straight historical fiction. Remember the names but no previous notions. This is Cornwell's unique concept of the Arthurian characters. It took me about a quarter of the novel to get used to his portrayals; but once I did, I was engrossed.<br /><br />Derfel, formerly one of Arthur's warriors, is an old man and has become a monk. He, with the help and questions of the present Queen of Powys, Igraine, is writing his memories of what transpired years before. The story was engaging, and the descriptions were vivid, especially the battle and fight scenes. I take special note of preparation for the final battle with Powys and the battle itself. I think fight/battle scenes are Cornwell's strength. The physical descriptions of the characters were complete and well done. The conclusion was satisfying but did leave room for a continuation of the story.<br /><br /> I did not like either Arthur or Guinevere. As one character puts it: "His [Arthur's] soul is a chariot drawn by two horses; ambition and conscience ... the horse of ambition is in the right-hand harness and it will always outpull the other." I felt this Arthur was too perfect and Machiavellian at times. Guinevere was an opportunist, selfish, and, I felt, hypocritical. I liked Cornwell's involving Culhwch, from the <a title="The Mabinogion by Jeffrey Gantz" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/455219.The_Mabinogion">The Mabinogion</a> in the story 'How Culhwch won Olwen'. He was Arthur's cousin. In the Welsh tale, Arthur and Cei help Culhwch; in this novel, he helps them. I enjoyed Lancelot's being brought down a peg or two. The earlier part of the Ynys Trebes incident shows flashes of humor; generally, I thought the book was humorless. Merlin's personality was as I had visualized it: His often repeated mantra: "Fate is inexorable" is familiar to me from Cornwell's Saxon/Viking [Uhtred] series. I conclude Cornwell often carries over a certain turn of phrase from one book to another. Originality and creativity stretch only so far.<br /><br />The added material was helpful, although the place and character lists seemed incomplete. I wish the character list had been set up geographically, with the people named and described underneath the name of the geographic entity. I also wished there had been a 'family tree' of those characters related in some way by either marriage or blood. I never did write one down but had a sketchy view in my head. <br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed this book. I was undecided between 3 or 4 stars. I'm not giving the book the benefit of the doubt, as I'm probably 'over-Arthured', just having read another Arthurian book back-to-back with this one. This is a great addition to Arthurian historical fiction.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12307575115120149741">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_318966025">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318966025" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/595788-alex-telander" class="left imgcol" title="Alex Telander"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1326834904p2/595788.jpg" alt="Alex Telander" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/318966025?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 25, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/595788-alex-telander" class="user" title="Alex Telander">Alex Telander</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/595788-alex-telander?shelf=books-read-in-2012" class="actionLinkLite">books-read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer318966025">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5893913202623591281">There have been many books written about the legend of King Arthur, the knights of the Round Table, Lancelot and Guenevere; a story that is known the world over and been talked about for more than a millennium. Some of those stories have tried to remain true to the original myth — though it still remains unknown whether there really was a man who went by that name — and others have gone off into their own world, using these familiar characters. Bernard Cornwell’s Warlord Chronicles is one that r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5893913202623591281">There have been many books written about the legend of King Arthur, the knights of the Round Table, Lancelot and Guenevere; a story that is known the world over and been talked about for more than a millennium. Some of those stories have tried to remain true to the original myth — though it still remains unknown whether there really was a man who went by that name — and others have gone off into their own world, using these familiar characters. Bernard Cornwell’s Warlord Chronicles is one that remains relatively true to the heart of the story, while at the same time revealing the strong themes of Britain in the fifth century, and in so doing, has become one of the main canons of Arthurian literature.<br /><br />The story of the rise of Arthur and his helping of Britain begins with its narrator, Derfel, now an old monk working away in a decaying monastery. He professes to his Bishop Sansum that he is writing the story of Jesus Christ the savior in the words of the Britons so they may better understand him, when he is really recording the story of the greatest warrior to ever live, so it may be well and correctly remembered for future generations, as compared to the tales and songs the bards sing where the true heroes are not always recognized and appreciated.<br /><br />The story begins with King Uther who does not have a certain heir and is not doing well. His son and crown prince was killed in a recent battle against the Saxons, while his bastard son, Arthur, has been sent to Armorica. But he has a grandson, who is also named Mordred, and who he decrees is the heir to Britain, and shortly after Uther dies. Derfel is an orphan living in Merlin’s commune, only the renowned magician hasn’t been seen in many years, and it is rumored he is in search of the lost treasures of Britain. Mordred and his mother are brought to Merlin’s commune where he is to be raised and educated under the Druidic religion.<br /><br />Arthur comes back with his men just in time to stop King Gundleus of Siluria from attempting to kill Mordred and take over the throne of Dumnomia. While Mordred is raised and educated, Arthur essentially rules Dumnomia, looking to unite the British kingdoms as one against the Saxons who are looking to take more land. Then he meets Guenevere and his heart is stolen, as well as the meeting of the character of Lancelot, who is renowned as a great fighter and warrior, though it seems the man is actually a coward.<br /><br />The Winter King is a great start to the trilogy that furthers the story, but has plenty of fascinating subplots that Cornwell is renowned for in his historical fiction, such as the Isle of the Dead, where the mad are left to roam and where Derfel must find a woman he loves. In the afterword, Cornwell explains what history there is to work from, and why he went the way he did with his particular story. Thankfully, the book doesn’t end on too much of a cliffhanger, setting up well for its sequel, Enemy of God.<br /><br />Originally written on March 17, 2012 ©Alex C. Telander.<br /><br />For more reviews and exclusive interviews, go to <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.bookbanter.net">BookBanter</a>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5893913202623591281">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4960789277769600038">With all three books of this series, Cornwell manages to walk a masterful line here between historical realism and magical interplay. It's kinda left up to the reader just how 'magic' Merlin is, for example. No firebolts here. Just when you think its all trickery... theres a kinda suggestion of something else. I really enjoyed that.<br /><br />Arthur was great too, and the take on Lancelot and others. I laugh when I see reviews questioning the motivations and actions of these characters. 'how could he be so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4960789277769600038">With all three books of this series, Cornwell manages to walk a masterful line here between historical realism and magical interplay. It's kinda left up to the reader just how 'magic' Merlin is, for example. No firebolts here. Just when you think its all trickery... theres a kinda suggestion of something else. I really enjoyed that.<br /><br />Arthur was great too, and the take on Lancelot and others. I laugh when I see reviews questioning the motivations and actions of these characters. 'how could he be so stupid' etc, as if some of the most intelligent human beings of reality, maybe such as we, aren't full to the brim of stupid calls on people and silly inconsistancies throughout their lives...<br /><br />This series was so good, I have closed the door on Arthur in fiction. My Arthur will forever be Cornwell's Arthur. </span>

              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1279146994">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13256758122165708364">This was just okay for me....I will add a star because I did the audio and it was well done. The reader did a great acting job. <br /><br />This wasn't my kind of book. All the casting of curses, the superstitions...are not my thing.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText13256758122165708364">This was just okay for me....I will add a star because I did the audio and it was well done. The reader did a great acting job. <br /><br />This wasn't my kind of book. All the casting of curses, the superstitions...are not my thing. This was heavy on the narrative, but the plot construction seemed well thought out. It was also heavy on the violence and fighting and not so much on the characters and their developmental process. The men just seemed like boys in mens' clothing acting out on the battle field, fighting over stupid stuff.<br /><br />This was a different take on King Arthur and Merlin. I admire the author's initiative but it didn't work for me. The male MC just seemed a little too perfect. He was always the savior or always being saved in the nick of time. He was always sought out and rewarded, yada yada yada. And of course that leads to the next thing.....all women were whores. And not only that, we are told several times (over and over and over) that they were whores. "I got it. I got it already," seemed to turn into an often repeated mantra of mine. Enough already. If they deleted that word, this book would be at least 24 pages shorter.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_526757517">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526757517" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8809317-joel" class="left imgcol" title="Joel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1406312808p2/8809317.jpg" alt="Joel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/526757517?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8809317-joel" class="user" title="Joel">Joel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18885916-the-winter-king" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8809317-joel?shelf=audiobooks" class="actionLinkLite">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8809317-joel?shelf=hard-copy" class="actionLinkLite">hard-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer526757517">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8565278928987766586">The Winter King is a book that I've had circled on my 'to-read' for literally around two years - when I'd first read it's description, I found it to be one of the more fascinating concepts on my list. I never grew up much for Arthurian lore, aside from your typical sword in the stone business, however medieval Britain has always been an interest, and I wanted to see if a talented author could re-write something that has been told so many times, and make it fresh and interesting.<br /><br />The tale is large</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8565278928987766586">The Winter King is a book that I've had circled on my 'to-read' for literally around two years - when I'd first read it's description, I found it to be one of the more fascinating concepts on my list. I never grew up much for Arthurian lore, aside from your typical sword in the stone business, however medieval Britain has always been an interest, and I wanted to see if a talented author could re-write something that has been told so many times, and make it fresh and interesting.<br /><br />The tale is largely one of Arthur, however covers much more - battles for the throne, the war between Britain and the Saxons, the in-fighting amongst the British, the battle between traditional Druidry and Christianity. This is not Arthur and the Knights of The Round Table - Arthur is not yet king, and his battles are on a much more grand scale. <br /><br />The story is told from a first person point of view, from Derfel, a fairly common man who rises through the military and social ranks, and befriends Arthur. It's a unique viewpoint, as Derfel has his own unique story, but additionally he's giving a point of view of the happenings of the court, the battles, the discussions and debates; all from the outside looking in. Derfel is an enjoyable character and narrator, a man of honor and loyalty but not black/white. <br /><br />The book is fairly clean, but also gritty and realistic. Finer points of the lives in this time period are touched on, and it isn't the sugar-coated family-friendly Arthur stories that people are accustomed to. The prose is approachable, yet very well suited to the story being told. The dialogue is realistic, well-moving, well-placed; characters all have their own unique voices and speech styles, their words and actions are convincing and moving, and the overall story is well told. The battle scenes, while not numerous, are exciting and flowing, and the larger dialogue sections do not get bogged down or too heavy for their own good.<br /><br />Overall, The Winter King grew on me more and more with each page turn, and by the end I found that I had thoroughly enjoyed it - about as much as I'd hoped to over the years of waiting to read it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8565278928987766586">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_88273567">

  <a href="/user/show/2735124-cara" class="left imgcol" title="Cara"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Cara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/88273567?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2735124-cara" class="user" title="Cara">Cara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2735124-cara?shelf=arthurian" class="actionLinkLite">arthurian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer88273567">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13042540404840179355">As a fictionalized account of the final attempt of the Britons to defend their land from invading Saxons and Irish, this is an engaging tale, with likeable heroes. As a tale of Arthur, it disappoints. <br /><br />The major problem with Cornwell's characterization is that the villains are without redeeming qualities. They are invariably a combination of the following qualities: brutal, vicious, duplicitous, selfish, and cowardly. This makes Arthur come off as particularly obtuse. For example, I don't have a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13042540404840179355">As a fictionalized account of the final attempt of the Britons to defend their land from invading Saxons and Irish, this is an engaging tale, with likeable heroes. As a tale of Arthur, it disappoints. <br /><br />The major problem with Cornwell's characterization is that the villains are without redeeming qualities. They are invariably a combination of the following qualities: brutal, vicious, duplicitous, selfish, and cowardly. This makes Arthur come off as particularly obtuse. For example, I don't have a problem with Cornwell re-writing Lancelot to make him ignoble, but to make him as wholly reprehensible as he is, and to have every credible character except Arthur realize what a useless and narcissistic person he is, just makes Arthur look stupid. It's hard to imagine how such a man could be a great leader of soldiers in an age of social and religious conflict when he can't seem to judge them well.<br /><br />Arthur's poor judgment, at least when it comes to people, is problematic in this work, but it completely drains the second book, Enemy of God, of the tragedy that is inherent in the Arthurian story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13042540404840179355">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_153242638">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153242638" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library" class="left imgcol" title="Lolly's Library"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450680358p2/2649865.jpg" alt="Lolly's Library" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/153242638?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 12, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library" class="user" title="Lolly's Library">Lolly's Library</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=arthurian-legend" class="actionLinkLite">arthurian-legend</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=part-of-a-series" class="actionLinkLite">part-of-a-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer153242638">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17970965992765858694">This is not a nice book. This is not a tale of King Arthur of which Disney would approve. It's not romantic, glossy, subtle, or sanitized. There are no chivalrous knights, the kind which spring from the pages of <a title="Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table by Thomas Malory" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/672875.Malory_s_Le_Morte_d_Arthur_King_Arthur_and_the_Legends_of_the_Round_Table">Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur: King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table</a>. There are no lessons of magical shape-shifting as in T.H. White's <a title="The Once and Future King by T.H. White" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1396008.The_Once_and_Future_King">The Once and Future King</a>. And there are certainly no rites celebrating the strength of female divinity as portrayed in Marion Zimmer Bradley's <a title="The Mists of Avalon (Avalon, #1) by Marion Zimmer Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/402045.The_Mists_of_Avalon__Avalon___1_">The</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17970965992765858694">This is not a nice book. This is not a tale of King Arthur of which Disney would approve. It's not romantic, glossy, subtle, or sanitized. There are no chivalrous knights, the kind which spring from the pages of <a title="Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table by Thomas Malory" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/672875.Malory_s_Le_Morte_d_Arthur_King_Arthur_and_the_Legends_of_the_Round_Table">Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur: King Arthur and the Legends of the Round Table</a>. There are no lessons of magical shape-shifting as in T.H. White's <a title="The Once and Future King by T.H. White" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1396008.The_Once_and_Future_King">The Once and Future King</a>. And there are certainly no rites celebrating the strength of female divinity as portrayed in Marion Zimmer Bradley's <a title="The Mists of Avalon (Avalon, #1) by Marion Zimmer Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/402045.The_Mists_of_Avalon__Avalon___1_">The Mists of Avalon</a>.<br /><br />This is a down-and-dirty book. It's brutish, thuggish, full of crude men, cruel battles, harsh realities and bleak futures. Set in the dawn of the Dark Ages, the days when Rome has been crushed, its influences eclipsed and all traces of Roman law and infrastructure are being savagely snuffed out by the sweeping hordes of Vandals and Visigoths, Saxons and Franks, <strong>The Winter King</strong> is a tale of a time when life is short and one's value is determined, if you're a man, by the strength of your sword arm and, if you're a women, by the power and wealth behind your father's name. It is a story of the one man devoted to peace in this time of chaos. Arthur, some say king of Britain, some say warlord, a man of myth and legend who manages to come to new life between the pages of Cornwell's novel.<br /><br />Yet, despite the barbaric nature of the story which unfolds in <strong>The Winter King</strong>, there's nothing gratuitous about it. The opposite, in fact. Everything, from the death of a soldier to the rape of a captive woman to the sacrifice of a prisoner of war is carried out in the most casual, matter-of-fact manner, a method of storytelling which only enhances the tale's sense of realism, giving the narrative a level of depth and gravity missing from many books which glorify the spilling of blood and go into almost gleeful detail whenever degradation or humiliation occur. In this manner, Cornwell accurately and vividly paints a portrait of a land in turmoil, where all traces of Rome's colonization are slowly being eroded, pillaged, buried and forgotten. A land under constant threat from marauders without and treachery within, where your ally today can become your enemy tomorrow for the right amount of gold. A land convulsed by religious dissent just as much as it was by invasion and politics, its Old Gods fighting for supremacy with the new god of the White Christ, pagan against Christian, magic against piety.<br /><br />Though Cornwell is a talented tale-weaver, there are a couple of nitpicks I have with the book. First off, he tends to repeat himself, giving a description of someone or something only to repeat it a couple of paragraphs later. Now, if the repetition occurred at a greater distance, with the vast and often complex array of place-names, characters and descriptions, that reminder of who rules where and invaded or conspired with which tribe can be a much needed aid. However, even I, the most addlepated of blondes, can remember information long enough not to need a refresher of said information three paragraphs later. Second, the spitting. Everyone. Spits. A lot. Whenever I put the book down for a break, I felt as though I needed to take a bath from all the flying phlegm. Now I'm sure all the spitting, to ward off evil, to seal a curse, to call on lucky spirits, to show disdain, is historically accurate or at least appropriate, but after a while it was just plain gross and distracting. Hell, I would've settled for a few bouts of pissing or defecation in place of the spitting, just to break the mucus monotony.<br /><br />However, despite those little quirks, which did tend to ease up towards the end of the book, this was a well-written, fast-paced, compellingly-told story. The battle scenes were written in an almost terse manner, revealing the carnage and destruction wrought without reveling in it. The dialogue never felt stilted or awkward, which can sometimes occur in the best of adventure novels, making character interactions feel real and absolutely human. And Cornwell has a deft hand at piecing together the often disparate Roman and Ancient British place names with the even more prehistoric monuments and landscape features, giving the reader an almost tangible sense of that isle's immense history. As much as I've enjoyed reading other interpretations of Arthurian history, those with a feminist slant, those with a more modern, Americanized, "democracy for all" slant, Cornwell's novel is the one which feels as though it were the actual truth, a factual account of this shadowy period of history which somehow became lost to time.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17970965992765858694">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/2570383-kerry" class="left imgcol" title="Kerry"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449027374p2/2570383.jpg" alt="Kerry" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/393840967?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2570383-kerry" class="user" title="Kerry">Kerry</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2570383-kerry?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2570383-kerry?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer393840967">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer338467148287359049">Cornwell accepts historical information which indicates Arthur was more great warlord than great king. Cornwell's Arthur story is from the p.o.v. of a 5th century warrior named Derfel and he has a fictional life of his own. Therefore, Derfel's story of Warlord Arthur (The Winter King is Mordred) is more about the time of Arthur and less about Arthur's life around the round table (also historical fallacy it appears). <br /><br />As for other familiar personalities, Lancelot is an unproven self promoting crav</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText338467148287359049">Cornwell accepts historical information which indicates Arthur was more great warlord than great king. Cornwell's Arthur story is from the p.o.v. of a 5th century warrior named Derfel and he has a fictional life of his own. Therefore, Derfel's story of Warlord Arthur (The Winter King is Mordred) is more about the time of Arthur and less about Arthur's life around the round table (also historical fallacy it appears). <br /><br />As for other familiar personalities, Lancelot is an unproven self promoting craven pretty boy. Guinevere is more intolerant gold digger with queenly ambitions. Druid Merlin is self absorbed wanderer and he's willing to make any sacrifice to reassert power for the old pre-Roman gods. Christian beliefs have begun to take hold and its' followers have an agenda for a new god. An enjoyable surprise, Merlin's protege Nimue takes a prominent role. She is a collage of fanatic, sexy, dirty, girlish, vulnerable, manipulative, repulsive, loyal, vindictive, wizardly and crazy. An unpredictable wild card.<br /><br />I find it a refreshing twist, others see it as missing the whole point. This makes for better historical fiction but much less interesting romantic adventure with all the chivalry.<br /><br /><img width="300" height="390" class="escapedImg" alt="Arthur of the Britions" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380387983r/748307._SX540_SY540_.gif" /><br /><br />The 'Winter King' is a compelling story that takes place in a time when Druids and Britons still have a chance to drive out the Saxon invader. A time soon after the Roman Empire has diminished and receded back to Rome and Constantinople. Some 400+ years earlier than Cornwell's 9th Century Saxon novels. <br /><br />The Winter King (1995) <br />Enemy of God (1996) <br />Excalibur: A Novel of Arthur (1997)<br /><br />I can't help but feel many of Cornwell's heroes are merely Richard Sharpe reconstituted for a different age. They share the same mastery of battle, strong male camaraderie and 20th century attitude to women that makes his heroes irresistibly attractive to oppressed females in bygone ages. Derfel is no exception to that tradition. I suppose, Cornwell stays with what works.</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/294249536" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6463881-lucinda" class="left imgcol" title="Lucinda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1437664381p2/6463881.jpg" alt="Lucinda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/294249536?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 14, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6463881-lucinda" class="user" title="Lucinda">Lucinda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7297518-the-winter-king" title="Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer294249536">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17215291971471294030">The winter king is the first novel in the acclaimed Warlord chronicles trilogy that is set around the myths &amp; legends of King Arthur and Merlin from the Dark Ages. It is a book that sheds new light on the Arthurian legend which combines myth with historical accuracy and the brutal action of the battlefield with such spellbinding realism. You can tell that this book is going to be good just by basing it on the background of the author and how utterly sucsessfull he was with the no.1 bestselli</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17215291971471294030">The winter king is the first novel in the acclaimed Warlord chronicles trilogy that is set around the myths &amp; legends of King Arthur and Merlin from the Dark Ages. It is a book that sheds new light on the Arthurian legend which combines myth with historical accuracy and the brutal action of the battlefield with such spellbinding realism. You can tell that this book is going to be good just by basing it on the background of the author and how utterly sucsessfull he was with the no.1 bestselling Sharpe novels, so from a readers perspective i was already put at ease. It is convinsing, fast moving and extremely readable, that is a powerful and drematic retelling of this particular legend. The old-world magic of Merlin is brought vividly to life as you are swept away into a lost world of bloody battles, treachury and betrail that is gripping to read. This trilogy was by far my apsolute favourite books by Bernard Cornwell which left me breathless and wanting more, and as a fan of not only historical fiction but also the Arthurian legends and Celtic myths i was truly mesmirised by his work. For those readers who love authors such as Conn Iggulden, Robyn Young or Micheal Jecks i can imagine that you will apsolutely fall in love with Bernard Cornwell's style and all the intensly moving drama, compelling action and character-driven plot that this particular book embodies. If you have already read literature based upon the Merlin &amp; Arthur legends such as by writers like Jack Whyte (Camuloid chronicles), MK Hume or many others then Bernard Cornwell's Warlord trilogy will take it to a whole new and interesting level of development that is truly remarkable and most enjoyable. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17215291971471294030">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bshs6TD4HSGfUE0N3UHel16CZyWOKhyN2WQLeFkUG7G1lgaei/kub0190oBKpC2ekCh7GZmInwLojsmOiwau0Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wHcS0raD43pFFbD1LuNd+lqKLZgG3RdM1AVavT7JdwAbKXilDYLQNJc4L3i5Bq7zlCAxpBF/lMPl75hL7NvCYA==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><img alt="12542" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1240500522p2/12542.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer12320215277900972259">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his mother's maiden name, C</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12320215277900972259">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his mother's maiden name, Cornwell.<br /><br />Cornwell was sent away to Monkton Combe School, attended the University of London, and after graduating, worked as a teacher. He attempted to enlist in the British armed services at least three times but was rejected on the grounds of myopia.<br /><br />He then joined BBC's Nationwide and was promoted to become head of current affairs at BBC Northern Ireland. He then joined Thames Television as editor of Thames News. He relocated to the United States in 1980 after marrying an American. Unable to get a green card, he started writing novels, as this did not require a work permit.<br /><br />As a child, Cornwell loved the novels of C.S. Forester, chronicling the adventures of fictional British naval officer Horatio Hornblower during the Napoleonic Wars, and was surprised to find there were no such novels following Lord Wellington's campaign on land. Motivated by the need to support himself in the U.S. through writing, Cornwell decided to write such a series. He named his chief protagonist Richard Sharpe, a rifleman involved in most major battles of the Peninsular War.<br /><br />Cornwell wanted to start the series with the Siege of Badajoz but decided instead to start with a couple of "warm-up" novels. These were <em>Sharpe's Eagle</em> and <em>Sharpe's Gold</em>, both published in 1981. <em>Sharpe's Eagle</em> was picked up by a publisher, and Cornwell got a three-book deal. He went on to tell the story of Badajoz in his third Sharpe novel, <em>Sharpe's Company</em>, published in 1982.<br /><br />Cornwell and wife Judy co-wrote a series of novels, published under the pseudonym "Susannah Kells". These were <em>A Crowning Mercy</em>, published in 1983, <em>Fallen Angels</em> in 1984, and <em>Coat of Arms</em> (aka <em>The Aristocrats</em>) in 1986. (Cornwell's strict Protestant upbringing informed the background of <em>A Crowning Mercy</em>, which took place during the English Civil War.) In 1987, he also published <em>Redcoat</em>, an American Revolutionary War novel set in Philadelphia during its 1777 occupation by the British.<br /><br />After publishing eight books in his ongoing Sharpe series, Cornwell was approached by a production company interested in adapting them for television. The producers asked him to write a prequel to give them a starting point to the series. They also requested that the story feature a large role for Spanish characters to secure co-funding from Spain. The result was <em>Sharpe’s Rifles</em>, published in 1987, and a series of Sharpe television films staring Sean Bean.<br /><br />A series of contemporary thrillers with sailing as a background and common themes followed: <em>Wildtrack</em> published in 1988, <em>Sea Lord</em> (aka <em>Killer's Wake</em>) in 1989, <em>Crackdown</em> in 1990, <em>Stormchild</em> in 1991, and <em>Scoundrel</em>, a political thriller, in 1992.<br /><br />In June 2006, Cornwell was made an Officer of the Order of the British Empire in the Queen's 80th Birthday Honours List.<br /><br />Cornwell's latest work, <em>Azincourt</em>, was released in the UK in October 2008. The protagonist is an archer who participates in the Battle of Agincourt, another devastating defeat suffered by the French in the Hundred Years War. However, Cornwell has stated that it will not be about Thomas of Hookton from The Grail Quest or any of his relatives.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12320215277900972259">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/115813-the-winter-king">Quotes from The Winter King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“But fate, as Merlin always taught us, is inexorable. Life is a jest of the Gods, Merlin liked to claim, and there is no justice. You must learn to laugh, he once told me, or else you'll just weep yourself to death.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/18402-but-fate-as-merlin-always-taught-us-is-inexorable-life" class="actionLinkLite">184 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“I do understand that you can look into someone’s eyes,” I heard myself saying, “and suddenly know that life will be impossible without them. Know that their voice can make your heart miss a beat and that their company is all your happiness can ever desire and that their absence will leave your soul alone, bereft and lost.”</span>
